*yMessag[e front tie ^lQaLLi
In his book “Poems of a Jew,” Karl Shapiro
describes the synagogue in this way:
The altar of the Hebrews is a house, not relic
but a place, Sinai itself not holy ground but a
factual holiness wherein the living God is
resident. Our scrolls are volumes of the thun¬
dered law, Sabbath by Sabbath wound by hand.
This poem is especially significant this New Year
as we enter our new home. What the poet is saying'is
that the synagogue is not a holy place, but devoted
people. Our new home will be dedicated and conse¬
crated through the voices of men, women, and children
who come to pray, to study, and to celebrate the
cycle of the Jewish year.
What will our Synagogue be? It will be the many
moods of our collective life; the earnestness of Yom
Kippur; the ashen gray of Tisha B’Av; the joy of
Simhat Torah; the memories of Yizkor.
In our home we will know that we are not alone.
The heartbroken will rise for Kaddish, surrounded
by people who comfort and care, Bride and Groom who
merge their lives and destinies will know that a con¬
gregation shares their joy.
In our home our children will stand before God
and people and their idealism and spirit will rejuvenate
our lives. It is no small matter to fashion a future
together. In our home grateful parents will hear their
newly bom named and blessed, and in our home, we
will hear our ancestors’ voices as they echo in our
lives.
When our people feel the strain of so much of the
loveless world around us, when we are tempted to
succumb to the quick and easy choice, when the head¬
lines are black with cynicism, we will as always escape
from madness into the sanctuary which shelters our
lives.
Before the Ark which holds our most precious
possession, before God who is the noblest idea we
possess, before the bricks our fathers built for us, and
in the presence of sacred memories of those who passed
before us, we will stand and take new heart and
resolve.
Rabbi David Gaffney
in his study
Our home will be the real center of our people’s
lives and the bastion of their values. We will come to
our home, the synagogue, to confirm our common
strength; to fill the gap between what we profess and
what we practice.
We will come to the synagogue because it is here
that the lives of our people are forged. We will come to
find the turbulence of our heart and to find reservoirs
of peace. We will come to contemplate and be enlight¬
ened by the panorama of Jewish life and history. We
will come to seek the Spirit who put an upward reach
in our heart.
Congratulations to us all on our new home. We
are deeply grateful to those who provided the vision in
the past, and to those who labor and support in the
present. May we in the future share our joys, forge
our destiny and stand before God in our new home.
May none of us live in isolation. May we ever harbor
reverence — in our new home.
Rabbi David Gaffney

President’s Message
We, the members of the Jewish Center are the
recipients of a “legacy of excellence”;
our spiritual beginings as B’nai Israel Congregation
in the early 1900’s . . .
the development of a comprehensive Synagogue-Center
at Third apd Silver Streets for the entire community
in the late 1920’s .’. .
the continuing expansion of services for youth and
family in the 1950’s . . .
the commitment to quality Jewish education through
the Solomon Schechter Day School in the 1960’s . . .
and now our new home at 10101 San Jose Blvd!
On one site a magnificent 2% million dollar
Synagogue-Center will serve the religious educational,
cultural, social and recreational needs of over 750
Center families as well as the entire Jewish com¬
munity of Jacksonville.
My prayer for the New Year —
“May the words of our mouths, the meditation of
our hearts and the work of our hands preserve and
prosper our “legacy of excellence” for ourselves, our
children and for generations to come.
Be strong, be strong and let us strengthen one
another.”
Charles J. Mizrahi
President

TO OUR ADVERTISERS: MORRIS ANSBACHER
Our constant gratitude, not only for making the publication possible, but
also for the support they lend to our Youth Activities. We urge our
members to reciprocate with their patronage.
• A reference and guide for the Jacksonville Jewish Center membership.
• Serves over 7,000 members and advertisers.
• All proceeds devoted to the Center’s program.
• The only Jewish publication in the community listing all groups.
The Year Book is a summary of the various activities and program of
this Synagogue-Center depicted in pictures and words. We are grateful to
the many organizations of this community who have cooperated in provid¬
ing us with material used.
We appreciate the efforts of our staff and other members of the Center
who have devoted time and effort on behalf of our 1975-76 Center Year
Book.
JACK DORMAN
Asst. Chairman
FLORENCE ROBIN
Secretary
IRVIN GHELERTER

A. ROBERT GAST
Executive Director
•J3r*ef *^co/,c °f -^¿ctivities
Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held daily, morn¬
ing and evening in the Chapel. Friday Family Services, Sabbath and
Holiday Services are observed in the Sanctuary throughout the entire
year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish customs are
observed in connection with Circumcision, Naming of Girls, Pidyon
Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah, Confirmation, Marriages, Conver¬
sion to Judaism, Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with outstanding pro¬
gram of secular and religious content. Hebrew School, carefully graded
with intensive Hebrew content; Saturday! School, with a curriculum
including Jewish history and customs; Sunday School; Solomon
Schechter Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew School, 1st through
6th grade; Havurat Hanoar with Pre-Jr., Junior, Teen and Confir¬
mation Congregations and Minyan Clubs.
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of juvenile and
adult Jewish books; Adult Education* religious and cultural Jewish
courses; Music programs of religious and cultural Jewish music; Sis¬
terhood and Ha’Rayim, a fellowship of young families; Social activities
provided by Mr. and Mrs. Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation
Program (Olympic Pool), summer weekdays and Sundays for all ages.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United Synagogue
Youth, various age levels are served by Center Youth League, Charles
Palot Club, Scouting (Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts); Girl Scouts, Brownies;
Athletics, programs for youth and adults; Center Day Camp & nine
weeks summer program at our 36 acre site on San Jose Blvd.
Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . Swimming,
athletics, snack bar, available for members during the summer months.
KENNETH BOWDEN
Comptroller
FLORENCE ROBIN
Secretary
ESTHER FISHLER
Secretary
INEZ ACHORD
Bookkeeper

s we build a beautiful and functional new Synagogue Center
to be used and valued for today and for generations to come,
we are offered the opportunity of honoring loved ones and creat¬
ing a living memorial to those who have passed on.
Man’s span on earth is brief. We do not write our names
in sand . . . rather we carve them in granite . . . linking them
with enduring causes and institutions that have significance, and
open stores of inner beauty for those who follow. What better.
. . . more permanent, more useful place to perpetuate a name
than within the dignified setting of our Synagogue Center and
within the hearts of the children of future generations.
Memorials and Living Tributes are sincere expressions of
respect and esteem. They perpetuate the honor of a family
name — the good works of an individual — the memory of a
loved one. They will serve through the years as an inspiration
to future generations and thus make immortal the influence of
those we cherished.
Each Tribute will proudly demonstrate not only the ful¬
filled responsibility to the community and the generosity of the
f donor, but also his act of enshrining forever the name of a loved M
'one in the minds and memories of those who will enter the new I
Jacksonville Jewish Center.

WHAT IS YOUR FAIR SHARE GIFT?
Surely you must have asked yourself, “What must I give . . . what is my fair share gift to
the new Building Campaign?”
You, and you alone, know the answer. You must find it for yourself.
No one can tell another the amount he MUST give to the Building Fund. But if we are to
succeed in raising our goal, the standard of giving must be on a very high level. You must make a
gift greater than you ever thought would be necessary ... without a doubt greater than you are
considering at this moment.
A magic formula for success? There is none.
Some members have been blessed with more worldly goods than others. It is only right that
they carry more of the load. But they should not be expected to carry it all. Therefore, every mem¬
ber must give sacrificially.
What is sacrificial giving? It is giving that means doing without. Without what? Something
we need? No, never! But it does mean doing without something we want badly.
Your fair share is the sum dictated by your heart, your conscience; your concern for the bet¬
terment of the congregation. If there is any question in your mind about the amount you now in¬
tend to subscribe ... if there is the least doubt . . . then that amount is not your “fair share.”
You will recognize your “fair share” when you have reached it . . . deep inside, you will know,
for then all doubt will be erased. You will be able to speak of your gift proudly, without apology,
no matter how it compares with that of the man who can afford to give more.
Select a suitable Tribute or Memorial.. . . Fill in your pledge card with the information requested.
Your selection of an appropriate Tribute or Memorial should be made as soon as possible!
Remember that you are able to pay your pledge over a 5 year period and your gift is tax deductible.
Your commitment is required NOW!
In your heart you will know that you have fulfilled a responsibility to your congregation . . .
to your children and grandchildren . . . just as generations past have done.
Jacksonville Jewish Center
Building Development Campaign
10101 San Jose Blvd.
Jacksonville, Florida 32217
. . . Return signed pledge card as soon as possible!

A Resolution of the Board of Directors of the Jewish Center, April 11, 1975:
“The measure of a man is how he serves his
fellow man.
Hyman Selber, Chairman of the Year Book Com¬
mittee for over 40 years, has been responsible for rais¬
ing over a quarter of a million dollars for the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center.
This remarkable record of achievement is matched
only by the success of the annual Center Year Book
which has received national recognition as an out¬
standing chronicle of our Synagogue-Center’s activi¬
ties and programs.
The lives of thousands of children, teenagers and
adults have been enriched because dedicated indivi¬
duals such as Hyman Selber have raised the funds to
help make these programs possible.
With grateful hearts the Board of Directors, on
behalf of the entire Center Family, salutes Hyman
Selber, his wife Bertha, and his entire family, for an
unmatched record of dedication and accomplishment.
May Hyman Selber’s example of unselfish service
serve as in inspiration to all of us.”

¡Sisterhood
“A New Heart and a New Spirit” from the 18th
chapter of the Book of Ezekiel, is our theme for a
second year. The hope, the comfort, and the consola¬
tion that is contained in the message of this theme is
still very much needed in this troubled era.
The New Spirit promises that it will be a stead¬
fast one. It is this spiritual regeneration that is essen¬
tial in the contributing challenge of educating EVERY
member of the Jewish family, through the woman
who is OUR member.
We are told that Ezekiel regarded it as his mis¬
sion to educate individuals both as committed Jews
personally and as responsible members of the com¬
munity. Our call is for renewed Spirit, much as the
“Spirit of ’76” embodied the determined beliefs of the
founders of the United States of America. They worked
for their beliefs with steadfast determination, as we
must do in these equally challenging times.
In our education programs for the year; they will
help us to focus on the spiritual regeneration which is
the urgent need of our contemporary society.
This Spirit will most assuredly come along wPh
our “New Heart” — our Synagogue Center at 10101
San Jose Blvd.
Join us this year!
MRS. HARRY FINKELSTEIN
First Sisterhood President
MRS. RONALD POLLOCK
Current Sisterhood President

Besi Wishes From
SOUTEL REXALL PHARMACY
The only Pharmacy in the Jacksonville area that combines
LOW PRESCRIPTION PRICES with a complete
PATIENT PROFILE RECORD.
Question: What is a Patient Profile: Record?
Answer: A record maintained by the Pharmacy, similar to the patient
chart in a doctor's office, in order to serve patients better by
recording on an individual card what their prescription and
drug purchases are and when they have1 purchased them, the
price of the medicine, which physician prescribed them, the
patients' drug allergies, and any other pertinent drug infor¬
mation.
Question: What purposes are! served by maintaining these records?
Answer: A complete record can be supplied suitable for
1. Income tax purposes or insurance company major medical
drug records,
2. Retrieving lost prescription numbers,
3. Supplying the pharmacist with information to enable him to
help protect the patient from the possibility of a physician
or other medical practitioner, prescribing a drug to which
the patient may be allergic, providing that the patient has
fore-knowledge of his’ allergy to the drug and has conveyed
that information to the pharmacist for inclusion on the
record.
Question: Does your Pharmacy maintain PATIENT PROFILE RECORDS
for your family?
Answer: We certainly do!
JOEL BRESSLER, Pharmacist-Owner
4940 Soutel Drive
Phone 764-4503

CENTER MEN'S TEAM
CHAMPIONS IN EVERY WAY
A winning tradition has been the
legacy of the Jewish Center and
the Men's Basketball Team has
superceded our highest expecta¬
tions. In the first half of play our
Men’s Team won 11 games without
any losses and became the cham¬
pions of the Southside Church
League.
Coach Sam Leibowitz has done
an outstanding job in synthesizing
individual talents into a strong
cohesive team effort. Robert Can¬
tor is the captain and is a strong
rebounder on the boards along with
Paul Bloom; Ronald Pollock is
team manager and is responsible
for recruiting the team players;
key outside shooters are Steve
Strumlauf, Bobby Shenkman and
Steve Kadish. Additional strength
and depth come from Alan Chep-
enik and Lonnie Margol. The bal¬
ance of the team includes Ed Wit¬
ten, Harvey Goldstein, Scott Eber,
George Green and Dr. Harold
Baumgarten.

Super Sunday . .

afternoon provided a dazzling dis¬

The Men’s team of the Center de¬

while Steven Strumlauf scored 22
points for the winners.

and 3rd grade DIVISION defeated
a hustling Orange team 16-0. The
champions are: Todd Berman,

lissa Roey, Bradley Rosenthal and
Gila Schindler.

raxfim
(YOUNG FAMILY FELLOWSHIP)
Ha’Rayim, which is the young family fellowship
of the Jacksonville Jewish Center, has been in exist¬
ence for less than a year. Yet each function it sponsors
is more successful than the last.
Ha’Rayim (the Friends) is based on the principle
that religious and intellectual expression may best take
place in a social context. We combined a sensual
Havdalah service with a stimulating discussion with a
marriage counsellor. We topped off the evening by
making our own sundaes.
Our Sangria Smash and Luau were great successes
as was our Jewish Identity Program and our Picnics.
Ha’Rayim continues to attract newcomers to the
community. If you know of any young family that
have recently moved to Jacksonville, let us know.
We’re the perfect group for them.
a very special way, we learn together, pray together,
cook together, sing together and holiday together. An
exciting year is being planned for young marrieds,
single parents, and young families of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center. Join us!
When you hear the name Ha’Rayim, think of
friendship. We are all friends sharing good times to¬
gether. Sharing our common tie, Judaism, together in
Shalom,
Isabel Balotin, President of Ha’Rayim

nc ij-jecjiinnincji
The Bible tells us of the Days of Creation —
the Seven Days of Prologue to the history of Man.
When God created Man, He said:
"Let us make man in our image after our likeness
and let them have dominion . . . over all the earth."
And God blessed the Seven Days of Creation.
In which day, then, do we live?
Ours is the Eighth Day of Creation.
We alone have been given powers of creativity
to change the world around us, to win "dominion over all the earth" and of ourselves.
What is the Blessing of the Eighth Day?
The Blessing is Man's responsibility and opportunity to join with the Creator in
advancing the drama of Creation. It is to mold the world in which we and our
children will live.
We have been given guide-posts for\ this —
enduring truths by which men can create\ their own best future.
WE HAVE BEEN TOLD THAT GOOD IS STRONGER THAN EVIL
This is to know that evil is only an interruption of the good.
And yesterday's evif is not stronger than the force of good with which we start
each day.
It is to; remember how1 much freedom we have to choose between good and evil.
WE HAVE BEEN TOLD THAT OUR ERRORS NEED NOT BE FINAL
At any point in our lives, we have the power to; regret a mistake,
learn from it, advance from it„ Not merely to be sorry, but to accept
responsibility for our errors, and for the hurt we cause others.
WE HAVE BEEN GIVEN A DIRECTION
Why is the world Man is building still so different from the world Man wants?
It is because Man is still so young.
Yet we know that a child can grow into true maturity, if he is given loving
guidance — a direction in life given to someone out of love.
This is what Man has been given in the Ten Commandments.
OURS CAN BE A JOYFUL LIFE
We have been given life itself: the edifice of our body and mind, senses and spirit.
The talent to love: to share ourselves with others, to belong to them, to live on in them.
The talent to serve: to seek; out the needs of others without seeking reward: to
know the delight which comes from the act of service itself.
With these truths, and the power that is in them, we can have faith in our capacity
to create what we seek most — meaning for our own life.
We can have faith in what we can know and in what we can do.
That is why in the language of the Bible, in Hebrew, the word for FAITH, and
the word for a man s work, have a common root:
AMEN
(reprinted from "The Birthday of the World," Davis and Ratner, Farrara, Strauss and Cudahy)

amp
PAST - PRESENT - FUTURE
1975
Campers Enrolled for First Summer
The Committee of the Center Day Camp reviews
the past and looks forward to a great future. The
Camp was founded in 1946 by a farsighted group who
felt that it was most important to have Judaic pro¬
gramming presented in an informal atmosphere for
part of the summer. To make the program more ap¬
pealing, the extras of swimming, crafts, sports and
games were added.
During the early years the Camp was called Yom
Tov. The Center was used as home base with a port¬
able plastic pool used for swimming. Field trips and
movies werei scheduled each week.
As enrollment grew it was soon necessary to rent
the facilities of a motel swimming pool and to add
many new activities to the all around program. A
baseball team was organized in addition to the exist¬
ing sports program and won many honors for the
Camp.
Eventually as more of our membership moved to
the Southside, arrangements were made to rent a camp¬
ing facility on State Rd. #13 which included a swim¬
ming hole, canoeing and boating. The wooded areas
surrounding the sight were perfect for hikes and na¬
ture studies.
In 1964 the Center Day Camp became an estab¬
lished Camp with a home of its own. The Building
Committee, with visions for the future, initiated the
purchase of 36 acres on State Rd. #13, containing
beautiful woodlands, a large colonial house and cleared
land for ball fields. To this was added an Olympic
swimming pool, combination tennis and basketball
courts, a double handball court and playground facili¬
ties including swings, slides and a large sand area.
As the only Jewish Day Camp in the area, the
Center Day Camp has continued to grow, to offer a
swimming program for beginners and advanced swim¬
mers, directed by Red Cross Water Safety Instructors.
Because the location is close to a lake and the St.
Johns River, we acquired a motor boat, joined an
American Water Ski Association and each summer
campers receive achievement awards for skills and
advancement in waterskiing.
For the past twenty-nine years our Camp has
continued to further the growth of the individual
child, to teach camping skills so a child could gain
confidence by doing and sharing with others, basic
ingredients in the art of happy living.
We have tried to utilize the natural resources and
surroundings in crafts and nature study, to help each
camper learn more about our Judaic heritage through
song, dance and dramatics.
It is gratifying to note that our reputation re¬
mains solid, that ex-Counselors and campers of the
past are now enrolling their children for a healthy,
constructive summer program.
With the new dimension of the entire Center lo¬
cated in one area, we project many innovations for the
Center Day Camp. We look forward to the utilization
of the Camp facilities throughout the year for school
and youth activities and to the constant growth of the
entire Jacksonville Jewish Center.

The Mr. and Mrs. Club continues to enjoy success
in its 23rd year. This is achieved through the enthu¬
siasm, co-operation and diligent efforts of its members,
who conduct the club’s entertainment and general
business.
Each committee is responsible for the entertain¬
ment for their month. A well-nlanned affair is pre¬
sented for the enjoyment of the club members. An
elaborate New Year’s Eve is traditional.
Friendship through the years portrays the con¬
geniality of the club and new members are always
welcome. Couples who are interested in joining may
contact any member of the Board.
Congratulations
members of the
JEWISH CENTER
from the Mr. and Mrs. Club on the occasion of the opening of their
beautiful new Synagogue Center at 10101 San Jose Boulevard.
May the wonderful cooperative relationship that we have enoyed over
the years continues to grow and prosper.

Philip Tennenbaum presenting a check to Charles
J. Mizrahi, president, for the Building Development
Fund.
U.S.Y. Regional Board Meeting November 16, 1952
What has this year been, in retrospect to the past
years of U.S.Y.? Why open with a question? Maybe
because this is the question of many parents and of
our own contemporaries as well.
The answer? Quite simple — We have accomplish¬
ed one main factor: Remember that so well known
phrase, “We, not me.” This is what I’ve tried to ac¬
complish, and it has finally become a reality. This is
what I feel is the main objective of the presidency, to
coordinate with his/her board, in order that those
people will extend the policy of “We not me” to the
overall membership. It was difficult for me, being in¬
different to grasp this concept at first, and still is, but
with the help of our advisors who were constantly
saying, “You’ll have no club if you intend on doing
it on your own, Karen.” How true it all was.
One thing that our group has realized is the need
for one another. You can’t do anything alone now¬
adays. You need to depend on others, which really is
not all that bad. There is a well know proverb, “A1
tifrosh minha-tzibor” — Don’t separate yourself from
the community.” Basically, we have incorporated this
theme as well to many of our activities. For example,
service to the Center on the High Holidays, River
Garden services, social action at River Garden, setting
up for Sukkot and various other programs and social
action projects, as well as showing a great amount of
courtesy to other synagogues and youth groups.

This year has been a fruitful one, I feel, for me,
the club members, community, and of course, our
treasury (thanks to Philip).
I want to close with one last note of seriousness
and importance. This past year, ever since the begin¬
ning of youth commission, under the direction of two
most dedicated men (George Strumlauf and Jerry Zel)
we have seen more involvement of our adults and par¬
ents working on the perpetuation of U.S.Y. and other
Center affiliated organizations. Unless, this process is
on-going and constant, I predict for the future that
we’ll have no U.S.Y. to speak of. It’s true, no matter
how many reasons we give. Parents are important to
U.S.Y. and to us.
Don’t wait for the Messiah to come one day to
give you advice when U.S.Y. is lacking in program¬
ming and membership, because in the meantime U.S.Y.
is in danger. Pm directing this to you parents and
USY’ers.
“You’ve got the whole Jacksonville Jewish Center
youth in your hands. Mold them in any fashion that
you wish. Just mold them while you can, while they’re
still moist, they’ll crack and go dry right before your
eyes.” This is important to me, all I can do is transmit
my feelings to you, and hope that our interests coin¬
cide.
Shalom U’L’hitraot! (Hope to see you soon)
Karen Mizrahi, President U.S.Y.

1935 FLASHBACK
a
roop

Troop 14 Diamond Ball Champs
During the Diamond Ball season Troop
14 entered a team in the Boy^ Scout
Diamond Ball League. William Katz
was chosen as captain, and Daniel Lipson
was alternate captain. The troop won
the championship of the Diamond Ball
League with only one defeat being
chalked up against them. They rang
up double victories over Troop 29 and
Troop 6, and a single victory over Troop
19. The men that caried Troop 14 to the
pennant were mainly William Katz,
Melvin M. Margol, Melvin 0. Margol,
Simon Rothstein, Daniel Lipson and
Irvin Surasky. Although every man on
the squad help:d to win games, these
were the men that did the most work.
The whole squad consisted of: Captain
William Katz, Alternate Captain Daniel
Lipson, Melvin 0. Margol, Melvin M.
Margol, Simon Rothstein, Irvin Surasky,
Alex Schneider, Bernard Sachs, Paul
Weiss, Clarence Berman, Raymond Co¬
hen, Murray Wolfson, Harvey Leitman,
William Schemer, Hilbert Margol and
Howard Margol. Wilbur Margol was
bat boy.
TROOP OFFICERS
Senior Patrol Leader
Scribe
Quartermaster
Librarian
Don Levinson
Mitch Crystal
David Greenspoon
Gary Kupfer
ADULT ADVISORS
Scoutmaster Charles Berman
Asst. Scoutmaster Leon Yergin
Committee Chairman Gene Crystal
Institutional Representative Henry Lasris
The Scouting program is more than just an or¬
ganization for boys ages 11 and up, but the new
concept of Scouting is designed for boys to develop
their own interests by leadership and direction of ac¬
tivities. The do-it-yourself program with the guidance
of Scoutmasters Charles Berman and Leon Yergin
help them to build a good set of character, leadership,
morals and citizenship qualities which are the founda¬
tion of future adult leadership.
Troop 14 under the leadership of Senior Patrol
Leader Don Levinson has done a terrific job of ac¬
complishing the goals of Scouting. All Scouts in the
Troop have advanced in rank and earned many merit
badges.
The Troop has been very active this past year
with participation in Scout Good Will Day, N. Florida
Council Mystery Jamboree of which they placed third
among twenty participating troops, “Unisex” Jamboree
held at thei Day Camp with the participation of the
Webelos Den of Cub Scouts and the Center Girl Scout
Troop, enjoying a cook-out and classes in Scouting
skills. These are just a few of the activities enjoyed.
The Troop spent one week (June 29 through July 5)
at Summer Boy Scout Camp at Camp Shands in
Melrose, Fla. and met throughout the 1975 summer
season.
The Scouting program is open to all boys ages 11
and up. It is a wonderful challenge and opportunity
for any boy who likes excitement and adventure to
participate in activities including the broad scope of
the outdoors, to the attainment of the Ner Tamid
award, a treasured religious award which ties Scouting
to our faith and to all the many activities that lead
up to the coveted Eagle Award. The Scouting program
has been adjusted to today’s youth — give it a try!

Cubmaster Commander Sidney Engelhardt
Den Mother Coach Mrs. Albert Levy
Asst. Den Mother Coach Mrs. Milton Mann
Committee Chairman Dr. David Mizrahi
Committee Dr. H. Ronald Levin, Alan Selmanoff,
Richard Wolfson and Daniel Ruby
Den Leaders Eric Berman - Webelos I
Mrs. Bernard Biller and Mrs. Harry Berman - Den II
Mrs. Norman H. Freedman & Mrs. Fred Ringel - Den III
Mrs. Chiel Wind - Den IV
Cub Scouting, as an introduction to the Boy
Scouts, continues to be one of the outstanding youth
programs offered at the Center. Boys between the ages
of 8 and 10 find many new and challenging experiences
in Cubbing.
The Cubs in each Den become a close unit as
they work together to develop new hobbies, take field
trips, explore nature and work on craft projects and
skits. The annual Rocket and Pinewood Derbies give
the boys the opportunity to design their own rockets
and cars and enter them in competitive racing.
In honor of Boy Scout Week the Cubs participated
in the late Friday Sabbath Services and the annual
Blue and Gold Banquet. To close a most successful
year all Cubs and their families gathered at the Center
Southside Site for an evening of swimming, sports,
games and cooking over an open fire.
The highlight of the year was the presentation
of the Larry Hacker Award to the Cub scoring the
highest in attendance at Pack Meetings, achievements
and good will. The recepient for 1975, a Cub most
deserving of this yearly award, was Andrew Ringel.
The name of the winner is recorded on a perpetual
plaque that remains at the Center and a smaller plaque
is given to the Cub to keep. The Committee extends
thanks.to the Donor who made the perpetuation of this
award possible.

s,
cont
162
This year has been so active for Troop 162. The
girls presented our sponsorship certificate at a Sister¬
hood meeting. They marched in the Veterans’ Day
Parade and had a Campfire with Troop 330 at Camp
Chowenwaw. A fun learning experience was the joint
Skills Day we had with the Center Boy Scouts.
Did you know that we sold more cookies per girl
than any other troup in the Lake-Man-Jose area?
The cookie booth at Lakewood did help. For Inter¬
national Day at Phillips Mall, the girls sewed their
own costumes, decoupaged bottles, and performed a
dance to represent Switzerland. They had a ball at the
neighborhood Games Day competing on teams made
up of all neighborhood girls. Our girls even got to
try their hand at sand art with Sisterhood help.
Have I left things out? Probably. The girls and
I would like to thank the mothers who pitched in to
help on various projects.
What are our plans for next year? Well, for start¬
ers, there’s a rescheduled neighborhood campout. We’d
like to see more participation of the parents so all
the girls could gain from their “expertise.” We’ll repeat
some of the fun things of last year and we’ll try to
explore many new and exciting interests.
Wendy Honigman, Leader

A Poem Written
For Deborah Bressler, Daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Joel Bressler
on the occasion of her
BAT MITZVAH — FEBRUARY 21 and 22, 1975
On this Sabbath day and Holy worship was great in the tabernacle and fellowship
The joy of the Congregation and parents was infinite inspiration as
the glass transparent.
The Rabbi tradition was a thunder on the mountain
As you drink a healing water from a deep fountain.
The Rabbi's blessing to the parents and Bat Mitzvah family
Is an infinite gift continually.
The Cantor with heavenly melody
As the revival increase to all the community.
How nice we all gathered at tables for the reception
For ever memory as carved on the rock for Deborah, congratulations.
We are Jewish honoring in our occasion as lighting the Menorah
Lord teach us to worship you and to adore the Sefer Torah.
In conclusion, Lord help the family for ever
Bat and Bar Mitzvah
As we sing joyfully the Jerusalem song
Hatikvah.
By Isaac Kabbani

“Let’s get our heads together.”
We have a new slogan and a new servicemark at Don L. Tullís
Associates. The symbol, shown above, illustrates in silhouette,
two people getting their heads together in the classic problem¬
solving way.
The new slogan expresses the way we, at Don L. Tullís Asso¬
ciates, have been helping people with their insurance problems
for years. Our business is essentially one of helping people
soften the rough spots in their business or personal lives. We
can’t do this without getting our heads together. That's how
we produce such nice results for everyone.
The new servicemark symbolizes what we believe to be the
most effective way to perform for our clients. When people get
their heads together, views are exchanged, ideas are formu¬
lated, plans made, things get done. This is the way we have
worked since 1960 and are convinced it is the only way to do
business. Let’s get together.

Mrs. Arnold Vandroff
Mrs. Michael Kersun
Mrs. Joseph Wilensky
The Jacksonville Jewish Community Council is a
viable, unifying force that encompasses the entire
Jewish community. Its structure reaches out to every
Jewish organization in this city and brings them to¬
gether in a policy-making group to help and aid the
Jewish people in our community, in our nation and
in Israel.
The Jewish Community Council was founded in
the mid 1930’s. The original aim of the Council was
a means of consolidating independent fund-raising
efforts by many outside agencies. It has grown and
flourished into a dedicated group of men and women
who have as their goal' the broadening of Jewish life
in many areas. This is obvious by the far-reaching
aspects of its committees.
In the .area of fund-raising, the annual Combined
Campaign has excelled in providing funds for the
United Jewish Appeal and local and national agencies.
Our Community Relations Committee has brought
together the Jewish and general communities through
the efforts of an active Speaker’s Bureau, significant
mailings of pertinent materials, and an Interfaith
pilgrimage to Israel in conjunction with the Main
Street Baptist Church to be held in November, 1975.
An Educational Series throughout the year fea¬
tures prominent speakers in all areas of Jewish life
and concern.
ARNOLD VANDROFF
President
Through the Community Programming Commit¬
tee, programs are planned for Israel Independence Day
as well as for other events during the year.
For the first time in 1975, the Council will spon¬
sor a Sunday Night Spotlight Series with four out¬
standing performers and speakers.
A Young Leadership Division has educational
meetings,1 and encourages future development and
leadership in the community.
An active year-round Women’s Division has been
a vital area of the Council’s program, featuring edu¬
cation as well as a successful fund-raising Campaign.
A Shalom Committee has been organized to meet and
greet newcomers to Jacksonville.
The Council also keeps a calendar for the Jewish
community for all organizations, which helps to avoid
conflict of activities.
Last, but not least, the aim and goal of the
Council is to extend the horizon of Jewish identity
and to intensify a desire for Jewish education to a
more significant depth on a community wide basis. We
endeavor to eliminate duplication, fragmantation and
its consequences. A united effort in this particular
field benefits all segments of the community.
WE ARE ONE

Jewish Family & Children’s Service has a 58 year
old history of meeting the needs of the Jewish Com¬
munity. Last year alone, we served 370 families and
assisted over 40 transients with lodgings, food and on
occasion rejoined them with their families.
J.F.C.S.’ principal service is counseling, marital,
parent-child or individual personal adjustment. Excit¬
ing plans for 1975 include three separate encounter
groups for singles, marrieds and students.
Last year J.F.C.S. oriented three Russian families
to the Jewish Community in Jacksonville. Most of the
members of the families are now self supporting,
speak some English (thanks to our volunteers), and are
indeed a part of the community.
dshildren
?rvice§
Lawrence Rackow
J.F.C.S. do® “what must be done” to help an
individual or family to function effectively. These ser¬
vices include—-but are not limited to—job placement,
education loans, camp scholarships, emergency trans¬
portation; housing, medical, dental or legal assistance,
foster care and boarding home placement as well as
clothing distribution to the needy and a small circulat¬
ing library for the housebound.
The agency continues to handle the local intake
for River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged. The
elderly are transported to their doctors, taken on shop¬
ping trips, and medication is obtained for them at
cost or no charge. Daily telephone contact is main¬
tained for the housebound clients.
Visits to the patients at the Northeast Florida
State Hospital at MacClenny and to the inmat® at
Raiford is a vital part of the outreach program.
J.F.C.S. is a non-profit agency supported by the
Jewish Community Council and the United Way.
In many interesting facets of the program, volun¬
teers are urgently needed. To help or be helped, call
J.F.C.S. at 396-2941,

This is the 29th edition of the Center Year Book
in which the Home reported on its services to the aged
and infirm of our community. As each year’s copy
was issued since 1946, the growth of the Home was
described in terms of meeting human needs.
Progress was seldom a smooth and even pace in
the development of our institution. In some years, we
covered great distances rapidly and at other times,
we made snail-like progress and even regressed.
But the overview of almost 30 years activities
reflects the phenomenal needs for the Home and the
similar phenomenal response from the Jewish Com¬
munities of Florida to meet these needs.
Mrs. Tillie Reiser directing the Players.
Thus, the first year’s description of a lovely co¬
lonial mansion, housing 12 residents with a staff of
eight, is quite different from the current modem struc¬
tures with a staff of 150 serving 180 residents. As
needs developed, the Home designed and created new
lifestyles to serve the aging, the infirm and the mentally
impaired. The track record for maintaining the senile
patient is outstanding by the very limited number of
Jewish aged ip the State Hospital.
The River Garden Players are well known for their
performances these past 18 years. But its fame spread
much further this year when they went on tour to
play before civic groups and organizations. The Players
were able to move right out into the community,
thanks to the Auxiliary, by the use of a “Chief Iron¬
side” van to transport wheelchair Players to other
stages.
The devoted concerns of a Jewish Community for
its elderly and infirm is> reflected by the quality of
services they* receive in the Home.

President Mrs. Nathan Gold

River Garden Auxiliary, formerly Ladies Hebrew
Sheltering Aid Society, is entering its 54th year of
“Service with a Heart.” River Garden Auxiliary was
founded by a group of dedicated women who dream¬
ed of having a home for Jewish aged in Jacksonville.
In 1946 their dream became a reality and the Auxi¬
liary continues to provide a home for the aged giving
them not only shelter but also loving care, companion¬
ship and medical care.
The Auxiliary provides extra budgetary needs as
furnishing, redecorating, air conditioning, kitchen, of¬
fice, and infirmary equipment, and hobby shop mater¬
ials. Funds are raised through our Tombola, Donor,
social printing, candelabra and mezzuzah memorials as
well as dues and life membership. Linen at the Home
is replenished through our annual Linen Shower in
January.
The Auxiliary contributes not only financially to
the Home, but also sponsors activity and volunteer
programs. Golden Sunshine meetings are held weekly.
Our members provide door to door transportation for
non residents to attend these weekly meetings. Resi¬
dents participate in projects! served by the Auxiliary
such as ceramic and art classes, hobby shop, and
resident’s magazine. Special parties are planned for
Chanukah. Purim, Mother’s Day, and Father’s Day.
The Red Cross Volunteer Program helps in yet another
way to personalize life for the residents.
As our new year rolls around, we pledge our
continued devotion and service to River Garden and
its residents in the hope that we can help bring some
measure of comfort and meaning to their lives.
1935 Officers of The Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society
and Home For The Aged
Best Wishes
NEIL R. SANDLER
The Bankers Life — DesMoines, Iowa

Mount Carmel Gardens apartments are designed,
even in the smallest detail, to provide comfortable and
pleasant surroundings. From the initial concept, Mount
Carmel Gardens has strived to create a living com¬
munity which provides much more than housing.
Mount Carmel Gardens offers a 17-story building on
a beautifully landscaped site, 24-hour staffing, com¬
munity facilities, and planned programs, all of which
were developed as part of an over-all concept ... a
concept responding to the desires of retirees for se¬
curity, comfort, safety, independence and the enrich¬
ment of stimulating social-recreational activities shared
with others.

The Progressive Credit Union was organized and
is operated to serve the financial needs of its members
consisting of men and women of the Jacksonville
Jewish community.
1974 was a good year. Many Jacksonville business
men and women used the service of the Credit Union
last year to supplement their operating capital. Loans
were made to many Jewish families to buy auto¬
mobiles, appliances, marry or confirm their children
and for other worth-while personal needs. 2ft Jewish

The aim of Zionist Organization of America is
designed primarily to engage in public relations in order
to interpret and improve the Israeli and Jewish image
generally before the bar of America public opinion. The
Z.O.A. stands in the forefront of those working to
strengthen Israel’s security. To accomplish this the
Z.O.A. has, in recent months, run hard-hitting messages
demanding justice for Israel in 27 newspapers includ¬
ing the New York Times and Washington Post. It has
also sponsored a nationwide speaking tour for General
Ariel Sharon — one of Prime Minister Rabin’s new
advisors — General Ezer Weizmann and Menahem
Begin, prominent member of the Knesset.
As you may know, the Z.O.A. arranged for Mr.
Begin to be seen and heard by millions of Americans
on N.B.C.’s “Meet The Press,” on radio and in the
CLreJit IA
nion
choice. The money advanced for school needs is fully
guaranteed by the U.S. government insured student
loan program.
All members receive free life insurance on their
savings up to $2,000.00. All loans are covered by free
credit life insurance for the full outstanding balances
of their loans. With all services rendered ratio charged
for interest is lower than charged by other lending
institutions in Jacksonville. Savers were rewarded in
1974 with 514% dividends. Savings are insured up
to $40,000 by the Florida Credit Union Guarantee
Corporation.
We extend a cordial invitation to all to join with
us and participate with us in the Progressive Credit
Union program.
Washington Press Club. Of utmost importance is the
fact that last January the Organization opened a new
Public Affairs office in Washington to make the Zionist
presence felt in the nerve center of foreign policy¬
making.
These and other activities of the Z.O.A. require
substantial operating funds. Financial support in the
form of membership in the Organization is therefore
vital. This year the Z.O.A. has decided to establish
two new membership categories: Patron Members at
$75. annual dues and Sustaining Members at $50. Reg¬
ular Membership may be renewed at $25. Please indi¬
cate your support of the Zionist Organization and
Israel by sending your dues to Irvin Rothstein, our
Membership Chairman.

Hospital on Mt. Scopus
October 1975 — The Re-Opening of
Hadassah Medical Center on Mt. Scopus
In October 1975, Hadassah returns to a new
magnificent Medical Center at Mt. Scopus. This re¬
turn is an historic event for every Hadassah member
and for the People of Israel! Most of all, it is a fitting
tribute to Dr. Haim Yassky and those who died with
him in the Scopus convoy in 1949. Hadassah returns to
begin another new chapter in its medical services to
Israel. This event indicates the lasting faith and de¬
votion of Hadassah. Mt. Scopus is a completely rebuilt
and expanded 260-bed hospital and Rehabilitation
Center. This will include a huge therapeutic Pool,

for all mankind.
Hadassah activities this year will highlight the
50th Anniversary of the Jacksonville Chapter.

JUNIOR HADASSAH 1933
Shir Li Hadassah is one of the three groups com¬
prising the Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah. We work
to raise money toward the support of Hadassah’s many
faceted projects and programs.
Throughout the year we have varied and interest¬
ing meetings, including speakers, demonstrations,
panels, films, group participation, cooking lessons,
study groups and on and on!
We are interested in the contemporary areas and
in working toward the fulfillment of Hadassah’s ideals
and goals.

The Officers and Operating Committee Chairmen
of this Lodge send greetings and sincere wishes for
good health, peace and contentment to the entire
Community.
B’nai B’rith through its many programs, has been
serving Jewry and humanity since October 13, 1843.
A.D.L. has been our watchman and our voice ex¬
posing the cancer of Anti-Semitism in our beautiful
land; also speaking out against injustice wherever it
affects Jews and Non-Jews alike.
Through Hillel, A.Z.A. and B’nai B’rith Girls, the
organization provides avenues for Judaic social and
religious experiences for our youth.
One of the purposes of B’nai B’rith is to provide a
service club type of fraternal organization for Jewish
men.
On the level of service to the total community this
Lodge provides recognition trophies, distributed to
teachers, selected by their peers, for understanding
and emphathy with their students. This understanding
and good relations with students makes for a more
productive teaching-learning situation. Jack Coleman
guided this project.
Another project is joining other service clubs on
December 24th, in manning the Salvation Army Christ¬
mas Collection Kettles. Jack Coleman was also head
of this project. This year we won the Salvation Army
award for the highest amount of money collected.
On the national and international level the follow¬
ing are some additional B’nai B’rith programs:
Adult Jewish Education program
National Bowling program
Citizenship program
Community Service —
Drug Abuse and Environment
Housing for Senior Citizens

MESSAGE FROM THE PRESIDENT, B.B.G.
Gators BBG has had one of the best years ever,
and with our newly elected board, it looks to be even
a better year. The new board is as follows:
N’siah — Marsha Proctor
Project s’ganit — Amy Winarsky
Program s’ganit — Babette Freund

We’ve done everything this year from surprise
breakfasts, creative services, First Annual Sweetheart-
Heartthrob Banquet, Helping out at River Garden,
putting on a Purim Play with Israeli Dancing at Mt.
Carmel, having a chapter leadership training weekend,
raising $580 for UNICEF, to hosting a North Florida
convention in Jacksonville.
Our members are the most active than they’ve
ever been. We’ve got two Florida Region officersi and
a North Florida Council Officer, and our chapter of¬
ficers are something else. Although they’ve only been
m office for a few days, many new plans are already
being worked on. We’re planning a Tri-city convention
for Sept. 19 here in Jacksonville, beach parties, a
bagel sale, very interesting programs and speakers after
meetings, and membership drives. It’s difficult to con¬
tact all the Jewish Youth in Jacksonville, so if you’re
interested in joining or just have any questions, please
contact Marsha Proctor, 733-8601 or Sandi Franzblau,
743-2304. BBG is Jacksonville’s Jewish Youth service
club, so please contact us because we want to help.

The enclosed picture is of the current 1975-76
officers of the Jacksonville Section of National Council
of Jewish Women.
Seated from left to right . . .
Mary Fink, Vice-President of Membership
Joanne Roobin, President
Helen Leibowitz, Recording Secretary
Sara Kofka, Treasurer
Standing from left to right . . .
Judy Paul, Corresponding Secretary
Thelma Nied, Vice-President of Public Affairs
Lois Schlesinger, Financial Secretary
Ruth Nachman, Vice-President of Administration
Arlene Zel, Vice-President of Program
Lauren Rothstein, Vice-President of Community Service
National Council of Jewish Women is an organi¬
zation which, in the spirit of Judaism, is dedicated to
furthering human welfare in the Jewish and general
community, locally, nationally, and internationally.
Through an integrated program of education, service,
and social action, it provides essential services and
stimulates and educates the individual and the com¬
munity toward their responsibilities in advancing hu¬
man welfare and the democratic way of life.
Our community service projects include such
varied activities as the ones! listed below, in addition
to our national and international projects such as
the Council Research Center in Israel: Mental Health
foEum presented each year in conjunction with the
Mental Health Association, Pre-school Vision Testing
for the prevention of Blindness, Service to the Foreign
bom. Supervising the making of layettes and pre¬
school dresses by the residents of River Garden for
the distribution to the needy, Tutoring slow readers
in four of our Southside Elementary Schools. H.E.L.P.,
and bringing a little joy into the lives of girls at the
Juvenile Detention Center.

American ORT is pleased to announce the elected

Our board meetings are held on the first Thursday
morning of each month and our regular monthly meet¬
ings, reflecting varied and interesting programming,
are held on the third Thursday of each month.
This year we will also be offering to our member¬
ship special workshops on Challah baking, Shabbat
Seders and home observance of our religious holidays
with special emphasis on young families. A few of our
fund raising activities this year will be our 2nd Annual
South Jacksonville ORT Road Rally (the winner re¬
ceiving a magnificient, well, maybe interesting original
trophy), White Elephant Auction and our Father’s
Day Lox and Bagel Box which was so successful last
year.
We look forward to our second year as a chapter
of Women’s American ORT with the ongoing convic¬
tion of promoting vocational education throughout
the world — “in the skills of work rest the dignity of
man and the wealth of nations.”
Anyone interested in joining us or who would
like further information about our chapter or ORT in
general please contact Cathy Kunsberg — 737-9097.

MEYER LEIBOWITZ POST #199
The Jewish War Veterans this year celebrate the
80th year of their founding. This veterans organization
is the first so founded and is the only Jewish organi¬
zation authorized to speak before the Congress of
the United States.
In addition to its many other activities, Israel is
the number one concern of J.W.V. working for peace
and security in the Middle East. No organization works
harder in the halls of Congress towards this end.
The 3 million Soviet Jews, separated from their
natural homeland, receive constant attention and sup¬
port from all J.W.V. members; we are active at the
national as well as the community planning and imple¬
mentation levels.
One local project this past year has been an
attempt to locate and mark all graves in Jacksonville,
of deceased Jewish veterans, regardless of affiliation.
This worthwhile project has resulted in a new attempt
by the local post to establish a new marker for all
military personnel that would be a permanent and
decorative attachment to the headstone for easy
identification. Hopefully this will be adopted on a
national level.
All persons who know of a Jewish War Veteran
cemetary unlocated are requested to contact any of
the officers of post 199.
OFFICERS
Left to right: A1 Sinoff, Senior Vice Commander; Arnold
Andriesse, Adjutant; Nathan Barber, Commander; Max Becker,
Quartermaster.
Membership in J.W.V. is open to all honorably
discharged members of the Armed Forces of the U.S.
including those who have served for a period of 90
days or more.

son ví
lie <J~Cevrah <3Caddish
a
JOEL BRESSLER
President
Communal fraternities for the purpose of provid¬
ing burial and its attendant rites for the dead have
existed among the Jews ever since the Talmudic period.
The Hebrew term for such a fraternity is Hevrah
Kaddisha (Holy Society).
As soon as the society is notified by the local
funeral home of a Jewish death, out of respect for the
dead, a Shomer or watchman is notified and sits with
the body, especially at night, until the time of the
funeral.
The practices of cremation, speeding up the de¬
composition of the body, or embalming, the slowing
down the process, flowers at the funeral or public
viewing of the body are all discouraged by Jewish
custom both universally and locally.
The Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddish was formed
in January, 1974, and has been functioning ever since.
It consists of about twenty men and women who
perform the necessary tasks on a rotating basis. Any¬
one who would be interested in becoming a member
of the local society may contact Joel Bressler, its
President, for further information.
<dCistory and ¿Z/dctivities of <J~Cevralt <3Caddisha
The membersi of the burial society used to attend
at the bedside of the dying person and receive his
final declaration or confession of faith (Viddui). They
would wash and dress the coipse, conduct the burial
service, and finally, together with the observance
and performance of the traditional rites, conduct the
actual burial itself. In the case of the poor, the
burial society performed its services without any
charge. In all cases, however, whether there is a
charge or not, the members of the society fulfill
their task as a sacred duty or obligation (Mitzvah)
which confers merit upon all who take part therein.
Since respect and acts of charity shown to the dead
were regarded by the Jews in all ages as the highest
form of charity and human service, the members of
the Hevrah Kaddisha have always been highly re¬
spected in the Jewish communities; in many cases
in the past they were accorded special social and
religious privileges in return for their important and
unselfish service.
The actual preparation of the dead for burial,
undertaken by the Hevrah Kaddisha, is known in
Jewish law as “taharah” (cleansing, purifying). Dur¬
ing this Taharah definitely prescribed ceremonies
are observed, accompanied by the reading of specific
prayers. The body is washed from head to foot in
lukewarm water, dried and then clad in the burial
clothes (Tachrichin). During all this ceremony the
members of the society preserve a proper gravity and
respect. To prevent funerals from becoming very ex¬
pensive burdens upon1 the survivors Rabbon Gamaliel
1, in the latter part of the First Century, ordered that
he be buried in a simple linen garment, and this
procedure, was soon adopted by the great masses of
the Jewish people. Men are in addition wrapped in
the same Tallit (prayer shawl) which they have worn
at services during their life but with one of the
fringes removed. As soon as the body is fully clothed
in the Tachrichin, it is placed in a simple wooden
coffin, again in an effort to provide uniformity in
the treatment of the. dead and to, prevent excessive
costs of burial.
If one wishes to honor their dead by purchasing
a more expensive casket, they are encouraged instead
to give the additional money to the deceased’s favorite
loved ones.
Mr. and Mrs. Neil R. Sandler - - - Wish To Congratulate

ROSH HASHANAH (the Jewish New Year), a solemn two-day
holiday when the Shofar (ram's horn) sounds forth in the
synagogue to call the people to repentence and to reawaken
responsibility to the Lord.
* All days of the Jewish year begin at sundown. Therefore, each
holiday really begins at sundown of the day preceding.

Rosh Hodesh lyar
30 NISAN
PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread — an eight-day celebration of the deliverance of the Jews from
Egyptian slavery. During the traditional Seder meal, on the first two nigfits of Passover, Jewish families read the Hag-
gadah (a ritual recitation of ttie story of the Exodus). Throughout the Passover periods, foods are selected according
to special dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews eat matzos (unleavened bread) to recall the hasty flight from Egypt,
when there was not time to allow the bread to rise.

ROSH HASHANAH (the Jewish New Year), a solemn two-day
holiday when the Shofar (ram's horn) sounds forth in the
synagogue1 to call the people to repentence and to reawaken
responsibility to the Lord.
'"All days of the Jewish year begin at sundown. Therefore, each
holiday really begins at sundown of the day preceding.

THE FLEET OF BUSES AND DRIVERS
For over 15 years the Transportation Committee has been involved in
operating a complex system of routes for the thousands of children who
come to the Jewish Center. With the completion of our new facilities in
the heart of the Jewish community most families will be providing their
own transportation.
The Jewish Center Family extends its deepest appreciation and grati¬
tude to Kenneth Bowden who directs the Center’s transportation system
and to Lewis Ansbacher and Herb Lewitt, Co-Chairmen of the Transpor¬
tation Committee.

Jacksonville, Fla. 32206
Dear Mr. and Mrs. Replogle:
As the Rabbi of the Jacksonville Jewish Center I would like to
convey my sentiments and those of my congregation to you and your
family on the recent incident involving your sweet and courageous daugh¬
ter, DeJean. As residents of the City of Jacksonville as well as members
of the Jewish community concerned with peace in Israel, we were deeply
disturbed to hear that pointless hatred and violence had struck so close
to home. May I asure you that the Jacksonville Jewish Center joins with
you as common members to a community of pain, and that we join in
prayer with you for DeJean’s speedy recovery.
As a Conservative Jewish congregation, we were pleased to note that
DeJean was visited by representatives of the “Center of Conservative
Judaism in Jerusalem.” The Director of the Center, Dr. Pesach Schindler,
presented a Bible to DeJean and spoke about her faith and courage in
the most glowing terms. He described her and you, her parents, as
wonderful and beautiful human beings whose sustaining faith was ex¬
emplary in dealing with the challenge brought on by this terrible ordeal.
Some weeks ago members of our congregation, Mr. and Mrs. Jack
Becker, visited the Main Street Baptist Church. They were invited to
explain to an audience of teen-agers the story of the history and tradi¬
tions of the Jewish people. One of the teen-agers present was your
daughter, DeJean. We are ( therefore mailing you under separate cover
the “Book of Jewish Knowledge” and we hope you will present it to
DeJean on behalf of the Jacksonville Jewish Center and the Center for
Conservative Judaism in Jerusalem, upon her return.
We trust she will find the book a comprehensive complement to
the Introduction on Judaism she received from Mr. and Mrs. Becker.
We hope to be able to extend our best wishes to DeJean in person
soon. Until that time, may God bless you — may He lift His face
towards you and your dear ones and give you peace*.

The Tofield Memorial Library is a growing collec¬
tion of varied and interesting books with something to
offer every member of the congregation.
Library hours are every Friday evening and some
Saturday mornings after Services and on Sunday morn¬
ings. Books may be returned or checked out through
the school office on weekdays.
Left to right: Leo Meizlik, Minnie Schreiber, Mrs.
Nathan Shmunes, Mrs. Irvin Rothstein, Mrs. Philip
Selber, Mrs. Bernard Glossman, Rita Gendzier shown
in the Tofield Memorial Library.
A very entertaining Oneg Shabbat was held jointly
with the Center Sisterhood at which time a book re¬
view was given and reports made. The new Library
and resource center will give impetus to intellectual
activity and learning for the congregation.

===== PAGE 271 =====
3f á ese old walls cou U talk...
If these old walls could talk—what would they
say? These windows that have seen so many seasons
change—that have let the sun shine in and kept the
weather out, what could they reflect? These curtains
that now hang so precariously from their rods—what
secrets hide in their folds? The desks in the classrooms
—what anecdotes could they recite? How many brides
have primped in the lounge? How many nervous 13-
year olds have stood at the bima and chanted their
Halftorah? How many people have received advice, con¬
solation, encouragement, and counseling from our
spiritual leaders? How many Sisterhood members have
been greeted and welcomed at meetings by presidents:
Sophie Finkelstein, Lucy Rubin*, Florence Bono*,
Bertha Sager*, Rose Hackel*, Eva Chepenik, Eunice
Safer, Rose Witten, Rose Gartner, Paula Fink*, Sue
Becker, Clara Jaffe, Evelyn Mizrahi, Runny Thier,
Ida Cohen, May Goldie Stillman, Faye Jawitz*, Betty
Rose, Blanche Slott, Idlene Slaff, Shirley Schinasi,
Sybil Ansbacher, Horty Gendzier, Susan Kantor and
Marsha Pollock. * Deceased
How many latkes have been eaten at chanukah
dinners? How many vegetables hung in the succah?
Oh! What these old walls could tell And now we pre¬
pare to leave it all behind. But, what really is the
Jacksonville Jewish Center, and what is our Sister¬
hood? Brick? floors? windows? drapes or furniture? We
know that it is not! It is our spirit, our sense of be¬
longing. Our pride in our accomplishments and the
grand feeling we have as we know that the synagogue
which we unselfishly support with our hearts, our
hands, our time and yes, our friends, has been and will
continue to be such a vital part of the Jacksonville
Jewish community.
This is what we take with us! We take our spirit
and pride as we joyfully move to our new home. A
home we have built for ourselves, our children and our
childrens’ children. How many brides will wait in the
lounge? How many 13-year olds will take their place as
a member of the adult community in our new sanctu¬
ary? We do not need the answers of these questions.
What is important is that there will be brides, babies
named, Bar and Bat Mitzvot, meetings, latkes, the
kinship of a congregation, the support of a Sisterhood.
We leave our old classrooms, but we take our
knowledge with us. We leave our sanctuary, but we
take our faith. We leave with the echoes of so many of
our simchas and sorrows still bouncing from these
walls, but we take our memories. We pause briefly to
look back at the spiritual home that has served us
so well for so long. And then, with a sigh, we pivot to
look ahead—to go forward into a new building—with
plans for our growth and expansion both as a congre¬
gation and as individuals. As we go, let us not loose
sight of this fact: whether at 3rd & Silver Streets or
10101 San Jose Blvd., it is the congregation—the
Sisterhood—the entire membership—it is as it has
been for the past 49 years—WE ARE THE JACK¬
SONVILLE JEWISH CENTER!
by Arlene Zel, Sisterhood
Cultural Vice-President
CONGRATULATIONS
to the

ATID is the College Age Organization of Judaism sponsored by the
United Synagogue of America. It was formed and is led by college students
and collegians who want to live and learn Judaism and continue their
identification with the Jewish Movement.
ATID invites all college age students who wish to involve themselves
in Jewish life and thought.
Our Synagogue is very anxious to keep in touch with all of our
youth throughout the year so parents are urged to contact the Center
office and give us their address at school. Activities are planned during
vacation periods to enable our collegians to get together with their friends.
Jordan and Shirley Ansbacher serve as chairmen of the ATID Committee
with Shaul Baruch as advisor.

It’s tough to get
an executive’s attention.
Until he hears
about Estale-A-Graph.
Most executives are far too busy to talk
to an insurance salesman. At the office.
Or at home.
The reasons are simple. They don’t have
time; they’re not interested in insurance.
Yet executives are vitally interested in
personal financial planning and how to
protect their estates.
Estate-A-Graph is a sophisticated,
advanced programming system for
individuals with substantial net worth. (And
for those who are likely to build a large
estate!)
It logically organizes for the client all
forms of insurance, benefits, and secured
assets; produces a clear, graphic
presentation of how a client can gain the
maximum financial advantage right now
...and in the future.
Estate-A-Graph is a free financial
planning service. Designed to show the
function of insurance in estate planning;
designed to build confidence and trust
between Acacia agents and their clients.
That’s our marketing philosophy, and the
Estate-A-Graph is just one way we put it
into practice.

===== PAGE 289 =====
CCenter
1903 — 72 Years of Progress — 1975
The history of the Jacksonville Jewish Center dates
back over sixty years to the founding of the Congrega¬
tion B’nai Israel in 1901. At the time of its incorpor-
action in 1903 this congregation had grown from five to
forty families. When in 1907 the membership had in¬
creased to seventy-five, the congregation felt strong
enough to begin the “erection of a suitable edifice to
house its religious and educational activities.”
At the end of the first quarter of the century, it
became apparent that a Jewish Center was necessary to
revise the “deplorable indifference to the . . . education
of the young. The continuance of this”, said Rabbi
Samuel Benjamin, “would have caused the absolute ex¬
tinction of the local Jewish community.” To prevent
this he proposed the founding of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center which would serve not only one congregation,
but the entire Jewish community in all its religious,
social, cultural and physical needs.
And so it was that in 1927 the Congregation B’nai
Israel became the Congregation of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center under the leadership of Rabbi Benjamin
and Mr. Harry Finkelstein, its first President. The new
congregation, relocated at its present site at Third and
Silver Streets, became a Conservative Synagogue affili¬
ated with the United Synagogue of America. Since that
time it has played a “major role in the existence and
development of practically every Jewish group in the
city.”
The year 1947-48 brought further evidence of the
need for expansion in order to meet the needs of our
youth in the community. In 1950 Groundbreaking cere¬
monies were held for the Activities Building, and the
dedication was celebrated in April of the following year.
The dedication of the Youth Center marked the begin¬
ning of the finest youth program in this part of the
country. At the same time plans were made for the
complete renovation of the sanctuary. This part of the
expansion program was completed and dedication was
observed in May of 1958.
When the need for several more classrooms became
most pressing, the Building Fund Committee began to
plan still further expansion so that every pupil could be
provided with a proper classroom. This project was
realized in 1959 when the addition to the school build¬
ing was completed.
In 1962, through the efforts of the Land Acquisi¬
tion Committee the Center purchased a 36 acre site on
State Road 13, 1% miles south of Beauclerc Country
Club. In July 1974 construction began on the new
Jacksonville Jewish Center so that the congregation’s
growing needs may best be served. Thus the Center has
grown from a congregation of five families to one of
nearly eight hundred and has become one of the great¬
est symbols of progressive Judaism in the Southeast.
Raymond W Cohen
